<process_isolation>
CRITICAL: Multiple pipeline runs may execute simultaneously on this machine. `ps aux | grep method.py` matches ALL runs, not just yours.
- NEVER kill processes by name (`killall`, `pkill -f`, `ps aux | grep ... | xargs kill`). This kills OTHER runs' processes.
- NEVER monitor processes by name (`ps aux | grep method.py`). You will see other runs' processes and get confused.
- ALWAYS use PID-based process management:
  Run: `uv run method.py & PID=$!` or `timeout <seconds> uv run method.py & PID=$!`
  Check: `kill -0 $PID 2>/dev/null && echo "Running" || echo "Ended"`
  Stop: `kill $PID`
  Wait: `wait $PID; echo "Exit code: $?"`
  Monitor: `tail -f logs/run.log & TAIL_PID=$!` then `kill $TAIL_PID` when done
</process_isolation>

### [3] SYSTEM-USER prompt · 2026-07-21 15:10:07 UTC

````
<hypothesis>
kind: hypothesis
title: Uniformity Improves Sentence Readability
hypothesis: >-
  The readability of a sentence is predicted not only by average linguistic complexity (e.g., mean word length, mean sentence
  length) but also by the uniformity of linguistic features within the sentence. Specifically, sentences with lower coefficient
  of variation (CV) of word-level features—such as word length in characters, syllable count, and word frequency—are easier
  to read than sentences with the same average values but higher CV. Initial experiments on 13,129 sentences from WeeBIT and
  CEFR-SP datasets show that adding uniformity features (CV of word length, syllables, frequency) to traditional average features
  yields statistically significant R² improvements of +0.138 (WeeBIT) and +0.042 (CEFR-SP), both p < 0.0001. However, uniformity
  features alone perform similarly to average features alone on WeeBIT (R² = 0.226 vs 0.226), suggesting they provide complementary
  rather than standalone predictive power. The 'Uniformity Principle' operates because uniform information density allows
  readers to establish a consistent processing rhythm, reducing peak cognitive load. To fully confirm this hypothesis, subsequent
  experiments must: (1) use high-quality word frequency norms (SUBTLEX-US rather than NLTK Gutenberg), (2) compare against
  modern baselines (BERT-based models, LingFeat), (3) provide bootstrap confidence intervals for R² improvements, and (4)
  evaluate on additional datasets.
motivation: >-
  Classic readability formulas (Flesch-Kincaid, etc.) rely exclusively on average values of surface features. However, cognitive
  load theory suggests that the brain processes information more efficiently when the rate of information delivery is consistent.
  If confirmed, this hypothesis would provide a new theoretical foundation for readability assessment and enable the development
  of lightweight, interpretable readability formulas that outperform classic methods by incorporating uniformity measures.
  This is particularly valuable for applications requiring fast, explainable readability scoring without large language models.
assumptions:
- >-
  Human readers process sentences as sequential information streams where cognitive load accumulates locally based on word-level
  difficulty.
- >-
  Uniform information density (low variance in word-level features) reduces the peak cognitive load compared to fluctuating
  density, even when averages are identical.
- >-
  Surface linguistic features (word length, syllable count, word frequency) serve as valid proxies for word-level processing
  difficulty.
- >-
  The coefficient of variation (standard deviation divided by mean) is an appropriate measure of uniformity that generalizes
  across sentences of different lengths and difficulty levels.
investigation_approach: >-
  1. Extract classic surface features from sentences in a public readability dataset (e.g., CommonLit CLEAR corpus or WSJ
  dataset): word length in characters, syllable count, and word frequency for each word. 2. Compute sentence-level uniformity
  metrics: coefficient of variation (CV) of word lengths, CV of syllable counts, and CV of word frequencies within each sentence.
  3. Train lightweight regression models to predict readability scores using: (a) only traditional average features, (b) only
  uniformity features, and (c) combined features. 4. Evaluate whether uniformity features provide significant additional predictive
  power beyond averages using cross-validation. 5. Analyze feature importance and ablation studies to quantify the independent
  contribution of uniformity.
success_criteria: >-
  The hypothesis is confirmed if: (1) Uniformity features (CV of word-level features) are statistically significant predictors
  of readability scores (p < 0.05) in regression models; (2) Adding uniformity features to traditional average features yields
  a statistically significant improvement in predictive performance (e.g., R² increase > 0.02 or MAE decrease > 5%); (3) The
  improvement holds across multiple public datasets and readability scoring systems. The hypothesis is disconfirmed if uniformity
  features provide no significant predictive power beyond traditional features, or if the coefficient of variation shows near-zero
  correlation with readability scores.
related_works:
- >-
  Feng et al. (2010) 'A Comparison of Features for Automatic Readability Assessment' - This paper evaluates various features
  for readability assessment including shallow features like average word length and sentence length. However, it does not
  investigate the variance or coefficient of variation of these features within sentences as a predictor. Our hypothesis differs
  by claiming that uniformity (low CV) within a sentence is a separate, independent predictor from averages.
- >-
  Courtis (2004) 'Corporate report obfuscation: artefact or phenomenon?' - This paper uses coefficient of variation to measure
  readability variability ACROSS sentences in corporate reports, finding that high variability indicates obfuscation. Our
  hypothesis differs by applying the uniformity principle WITHIN individual sentences, claiming that low within-sentence variance
  of word properties improves readability.
- >-
  Eltanbouly et al. (2025) 'Trait-Specific Rubric-Assisted Cross-Prompt Essay Scoring' - This paper uses 'word_var: Variance
  of word length' as one of many features in a machine learning model. However, it does not test the specific theoretical
  claim that uniformity is an independent predictor, nor does it use the coefficient of variation (normalized measure) or
  test across multiple word-level features (syllables, frequency). Our hypothesis provides a theoretical framework and systematic
  evaluation.
- >-
  Genzel & Charniak (2002) 'Entropy Rate Constancy in Text' - This paper proposes that speakers maintain a constant entropy
  rate across sentences. While related to information uniformity, it operates at the sentence-level entropy rate rather than
  within-sentence uniformity of surface features. Our hypothesis focuses on within-sentence uniformity of surface linguistic
  features as a cognitive processing principle.
inspiration: >-
  The hypothesis is inspired by three cross-domain insights: (1) From COGNITIVE SCIENCE and Cognitive Load Theory: consistent
  information processing rates reduce peak working memory load compared to fluctuating rates. (2) From INFORMATION THEORY:
  uniform information density (constant bit rate) is easier to process than variable bit rate in streaming systems. (3) From
  ECONOMICS: the Gini coefficient and coefficient of variation measure inequality/non-uniformity, which in other domains predicts
  system efficiency. The core insight is that reading is a sequential processing task where uniformity of difficulty within
  a sentence allows the reader to establish a consistent 'processing rhythm', reducing cognitive effort compared to sentences
  with 'bursty' difficulty patterns.
terms:
- term: Coefficient of Variation (CV)
  definition: >-
    A normalized measure of dispersion calculated as the ratio of the standard deviation to the mean (CV = σ/μ). It measures
    the relative variability of a feature independent of its absolute scale, allowing comparison across sentences of different
    lengths and difficulty levels.
- term: Surface Linguistic Features
  definition: >-
    Readily observable textual properties that do not require deep linguistic analysis, such as word length in characters,
    number of syllables per word, sentence length in words, and word frequency counts.
- term: Within-Sentence Uniformity
  definition: >-
    The degree to which word-level properties (length, syllables, frequency) are consistent throughout a sentence, measured
    by the coefficient of variation of these properties across all words in the sentence.
- term: Cognitive Rhythm
  definition: >-
    A hypothesized cognitive processing state where the reader establishes a consistent pace of processing when encountering
    uniformly difficult text, leading to reduced peak cognitive load compared to processing text with fluctuating difficulty.
- term: Sentence-Level Readability
  definition: >-
    The assessment of reading difficulty for individual sentences independently, as opposed to document-level readability
    which averages across multiple sentences.
summary: >-
  This hypothesis proposes that sentence readability depends not only on average linguistic complexity but also on the uniformity
  of word-level features within the sentence. Sentences with consistent word lengths, syllable counts, and word frequencies
  (low coefficient of variation) are predicted to be easier to read because they allow readers to maintain a steady cognitive
  processing rhythm, reducing peak cognitive load.
_relation_rationale: >-
  Added initial experimental results; specified methodological requirements for full confirmation
_confidence_delta: increased
_key_changes:
- >-
  Added specific experimental results: R² improvements of +0.138 and +0.042, both p < 0.0001
- >-
  Noted that uniformity-only features perform similarly to average-only on WeeBIT, suggesting complementary rather than standalone
  value
- >-
  Added requirement for high-quality word frequency norms (SUBTLEX-US) based on reviewer feedback
- >-
  Added requirement to compare against modern baselines (BERT-based, LingFeat) to establish contribution beyond current methods
- >-
  Added requirement for bootstrap confidence intervals for R² improvements (currently only p-values reported)
- >-
  Added requirement for evaluation on additional datasets beyond WeeBIT and CEFR-SP
- >-
  Specified that cv_syllables was the most predictive uniformity feature (coefficient +0.150 on WeeBIT)
relation_type: evolution
</hypothesis>

type: experiment
objective: >-

  compute bootstrap confidence intervals for R² improvements
approach: >-
  Implement: (1) SUBTLEX-US Integration: Download SUBTLEX-US norms from Ghent University (~51M subtitle words), compute word
  frequency uniformity using SUBTL_WF or Zipf scale. Compare against NLTK Gutenberg to quantify improvement. (2) BERT-based
  Baseline: Use bert-base-uncased from transformers, mean-pool token embeddings, linear regression head, fine-tune with 5-fold
  CV. (3) Comprehensive Features: Implement LingFeat-inspired features (syntactic, discourse, LM) or Deutsch et al. 2020 feature
  set. (4) Evaluation: Run all combinations on WeeBIT, CEFR-SP, and if available, WSJ/CLEAR. (5) Bootstrap CIs: 10,000 bootstrap
  samples for R² differences (average-only vs combined, uniformity-only vs combined), report mean/SD/95% CI. (6) OOV Handling:
  Document CMUdict and SUBTLEX-US OOV rates, implement fallback (syllables package for CMUdict OOV; log-freq=-1 for SUBTLEX-US
  OOV). This addresses major feedback #1 (SUBTLEX-US), #2 (modern baselines), and #3 (bootstrap CIs).

type: dataset
title: Sentence-Level Readability Datasets
summary: >-
  Successfully collected and standardized 2 sentence-level readability datasets for the artifact objective of computing uniformity
  features (CV of word length, syllables, word frequency). Dataset 1: WeeBIT (3,125 sentences) - established benchmark with
  5 age intervals from Vajjala & Meurers (2012). Dataset 2: CEFR-SP (10,004 sentences) - sentences annotated with CEFR levels

--- Dependency 2 ---
id: art_zKX1_wXmedjn
type: research
title: Word features and readability methods research
summary: |-
  This research artifact provides a comprehensive methodological guide for computing word-level linguistic features (syllable count, word frequency) and understanding the sentence-level readability assessment landscape. The research was conducted to inform experimental design for testing the 'Uniformity Principle' hypothesis, which posits that variance/uniformity measures of linguistic properties within sentences may improve readability prediction beyond traditional average-based features.

  Key findings across 5 research phases:

  1. SYLLABLE COUNTING: Three main approaches exist - (a) CMU Pronouncing Dictionary via 'pronouncing' library (high accuracy, research-grade), (b) 'syllables' package (fast heuristic, lower accuracy), (c) textstat library (uses Pyphen, only 54% accurate per GitHub issues). Recommendation: Use CMUdict as primary with heuristic fallback.

  2. WORD FREQUENCY: SUBTLEX-US norms (based on 51M subtitle words) significantly outperform older norms (Kucera & Francis, Celex) in predicting word processing times. Available as free download from Ghent University. Google Books Ngrams is secondary option.

  3. SENTENCE-LEVEL DATASETS: (a) CLEAR corpus (~5,000 excerpts, grades 3-12, multiple readability metrics + teacher ratings, MIT license), (b) WSJ dataset (1,200 sentences, 20 annotators, grades 1-7), (c) OneStopEnglish (189 texts × 3 levels). CLEAR recommended as primary for hypothesis testing.

  4. FEATURE LANDSCAPE: Feng et al. (2010) evaluated 5 categories - discourse, language modeling, syntactic, POS, and shallow features. Their Table 5 lists 8 shallow features. Critical finding: ALL existing features are means/averages (e.g., 'average syllables per word'). NO variance/uniformity measures were found, suggesting the 'Uniformity Principle' hypothesis has novelty.

  5. STATISTICAL METHODS: For testing incremental predictive power - (a) Cross-validated R² difference with bootstrap CI (most robust), (b) AIC/BIC comparison (model selection), (c) Nested F-test (linear models). Recommendations provided with Python implementation examples.

  The artifact includes: (1) Detailed methodology guide with installation commands and code snippets, (2) Dataset catalog with properties and access methods, (3) Feature catalog from Feng et al. (2010), (4) Statistical methodology guide, (5) Novelty assessment for uniformity features, (6) Experimental design recommendations.

  Sources: 14 references covering PyPI packages, academic papers (Feng et al. 2010, Liu & Lee 2023), dataset documentation (CLEAR, WSJ, OneStopEnglish), and statistical methods resources. All sources accessed and verified via web research tools.

### [4] HUMAN-USER prompt · 2026-07-21 15:10:07 UTC

```
A lightweight sentence-level readability scoring model for English text using classic surface linguistic features, evaluated on a small public dataset.
```

### [5] THINKING · 2026-07-21 15:10:29 UTC

```
Let me understand the task. I'm a plan generator for an EXPERIMENT artifact in the AI Inventor system. I need to create a detailed, actionable plan for the executor agent based on:

1. The hypothesis: "Uniformity Improves Sentence Readability" - which states that readability depends not only on average linguistic complexity but also on the uniformity of linguistic features within the sentence.

2. The artifact direction: experiment_iter2_dir2 - which asks to re-run readability prediction experiments with:
   - SUBTLEX-US frequency norms
   - BERT-based baseline
   - Comprehensive features
   - Bootstrap confidence intervals for R² improvements

Textstat Library (Syllable Counting)**\\nTextstat is a popular Python library (PyPI package) that provides easy-to-use readability metrics [1]. For syllable counting, textstat uses Pyphen by default for most languages, but switches to NLTK's CMU pronouncing dictionary for en-US [1]. However, a critical GitHub issue (#195) reveals that textstat's syllable counting can be inaccurate - for example, it counts 'faeries' as 1 syllable (should be 2) and 'relived' as 3 syllables (should be 2) [2]. The issue notes that 'Pyphen is only 54% accurate,' suggesting significant limitations for research-grade applications [2].\\n\\n**2. CMU Pronouncing Dictionary (via pronouncing library)**\\nThe CMU Pronouncing Dictionary is an open-source machine-readable pronunciation dictionary containing over 134,000 words [3]. The `pronouncing` Python library provides a simple interface to query this dictionary [3]. Syllable counting with CMUdict is done by: (a) retrieving phoneme representations with `pronouncing.phones_for_word()`, and (b) counting vowels with stress markers using `pronouncing.syllable_count()` [3]. This method is MORE ACCURATE than heuristic approaches since it uses actual pronunciation data, but it may not cover all words (especially rare or domain-specific terms) [3].\\n\\n**3. Syllables Package (Heuristic-Based)**\\nThe `syllables` package (PyPI) provides fast syllable Estimation using heuristics [4]. It is designed for speed rather than accuracy - the documentation explicitly states: 'For situations where accuracy matters, please consider the cmudict Python library instead' [4]. This is a key trade-off: syllables is faster but less accurate than CMUdict-based approaches.\\n\\n**Recommendation for Research:** Use the CMU Pronouncing Dictionary via the `pronouncing` library or directly via NLTK's `cmudict` corpus reader for research-grade syllable counting. Have a fallback heuristic (like the `syllables` package) for out-of-vocabulary words.\\n\\n### Phase 2: Word Frequency Norm Sources\\n\\n**1. SUBTLEX-US (Recommended Primary Source)**\\nSUBTLEX-US provides word frequency norms based on 51 million words of American movie/TV subtitles [5]. It significantly outperforms older norms (Kucera & Francis 1967, Celex 1993) in predicting word processing times [5]. Key measures include:\\n- SUBTL WF: Word frequency per million words\\n- SUBTL CD: Percentage of films in which a word appears (contextual diversity) [5]\\n- Zipf scale values (1-7 range, easier interpretation) [5]\\n\\nThe SUBTLEX-US norms are available for free download (Excel/Text formats) from Ghent University [5]. An online lookup interface is available at subtlexus.lexique.org [5]. Part-of-Speech tagged versions are also available [5].\\n\\n**2. KF-NAP (Keller & Caramazza?)**\\nNote: Search results for 'KF-NAP' primarily returned information about a clinical assessment tool (Kessler Foundation Neglect Assessment Process), not a word frequency database. This may have been a confusion in the search query. The intended reference is likely to Kucera & Francis (1967) norms, which are acknowledged to be dated and of poor quality [5].\\n\\n**3. Google Books Ngrams**\\nThe Google Books Ngram corpus provides word frequency data from books digitized by Google [6]. While comprehensive, it has biases (published books, academic texts) and may not represent contemporary everyday language as well as SUBTLEX-US (which is based on subtitles) [5].\\n\\n**4. NLTK Corpora**\\nNLTK provides access to various corpora (Brown, Reuters, etc.) that can be used for frequency norms [7]. However, these are generally smaller and less representative than SUBTLEX-US.\\n\\n**Recommendation for Research:** Use SUBTLEX-US as the primary word frequency source. It is free, well-validated, and specifically designed for cognitive processing research. For words not in SUBTLEX-US, consider backing off to Google Books Ngrams or NLTK corpora.\\n\\n### Phase 3: Sentence-Level Readability Datasets\\n\\n**1. CLEAR Corpus (CommonLit Ease of Readability)**\\nThe CLEAR corpus is an open dataset of ~5,000 reading passage excerpts with readability scores [8]. Key properties:\\n- Grade levels: 3-12\\n- Scoring: Multiple readability indices (Flesch Reading Ease, Flesch-Kincaid, ARI, SMOG, Dale-Chall, CAREC, etc.) plus teacher ratings (Bradley-Terry coefficients) [8]\\n- Metadata: Author, title, publication year, genre (informational/literary), licensing info [8]\\n- Access: Google Sheets (MIT license for metadata) [8]\\n- Use: Successfully used in Kaggle Readability Prize competition\\n\\n**2. WSJ (Wall Street Journal) Dataset**\\nThe WSJ dataset contains 1,200 sentences graded on a difficulty scale of 1-7 [9]. Each sentence was rated by 20 native speakers [9]. The evaluated subset contains 650 sentences with \\u226514/20 annotator agreement [9]. This dataset is commonly used for sentence-level readability assessment research [9].\\n\\n**3. OneStopEnglish (OSE) Corpus**\\nOneStopEnglish contains 189 texts (567 total versions) at three reading grades: beginner, intermediate, advanced [10]. Each text has three aligned versions [10]. For sentence-level analysis, individual sentences are labeled based on human revision patterns (sentences that remain unchanged across levels retain the lower grade) [9].\\n\\n**4. Weekly Reader Corpus**\\nUsed in Feng et al. (2010), this corpus contains 1,433 articles for grades 2-5 from Weekly Reader magazine [11]. Articles are shorter than other corpora (average 128-336 words/document depending on grade) [11]. This corpus is particularly valuable for elementary school readability research.\\n\\n**Recommendation for Research:** For testing the 'Uniformity Principle' hypothesis, CLEAR corpus is recommended as the primary dataset because: (a) it provides sentence-level excerpts (not full documents), (b) it has multiple readability metrics, (c) it includes teacher ratings, and (d) it is open-access. WSJ dataset is also valuable for sentence-level analysis with human ratings.\\n\\n### Phase 4: Feature Landscape for Readability\\n\\n**Feng et al. (2010) Feature Categories:**\\nFeng et al. conducted a systematic comparison of features for automatic readability assessment [11]. They evaluated features at five linguistic levels:\\n\\n1. **Discourse Features**\\n   - Entity-density features (percentage of named entities, overlapping nouns removed, etc.) [11]\\n   - Lexical chain features (total chains, average chain length/span, etc.) [11]\\n   - Coreference inference features (inference distance, chain statistics) [11]\\n   - Entity grid features (16 transition patterns) [11]\\n\\n2. **Language Modeling Features**\\n   - Unigram, bigram, trigram models trained on in-domain data (Weekly Reader corpus) [11]\\n   - Information gain-selected features vs. POS-only models [11]\\n\\n3. **Parsed Syntactic Features**\\n   - Average number of non-terminal nodes per tree\\n   - Average tree height\\n   - Frequencies of SBARs, NPs, VPs, PPs [11]\\n\\n4. **POS-based Features**\\n   - Nouns, verbs, adjectives, adverbs, prepositions\\n   - Content words vs. function words [11]\\n   - Noun-based features had highest predictive power among POS features [11]\\n\\n5. **Shallow Features (8 features)** [11]\\n   Table 5 from Feng et al. lists:\\n   1. Average number of syllables per word\\n   2. Percentage of polysyllabic words per document\\n   3. Average number of polysyllabic words per sentence\\n   4. Average number of characters per word\\n   5. Chall-Dale difficult words rate per document\\n   6. Average number of words per sentence\\n   7. Flesch-Kincaid score\\n   8. Total number of words per document\\n\\n**Predictive Power Findings (Feng et al.):**\\n- <response clipped><NOTE>Due to the max output limit, only part of this file has been shown to you. request via email\\n# For implementation, provide a script to download and parse SUBTLEX-US\\n\\ndef download_subtlex_us():\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"Download and parse SUBTLEX-US word frequency norms.\\n    \\n    The SUBTLEX-US file is typically an Excel file with columns:\\n    - Word: The word\\n    - SUBTL_WF: Word frequency per million words\\n    - SUBTL_CD: Percentage of films with word (contextual diversity)\\n    - Zipf: Zipf scale value (1-7, easier interpretation)\\n    \\n    Returns: Dictionary mapping word -> {'subtlex_wf': float, 'zipf': float}\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n    subtlex_path = Path('data/subtlex_us.csv')\\n    \\n    if not subtlex_path.exists():\\n        # Attempt to download from Ghent University or use alternative source\\n        # Method 1: Check if HuggingFace has SUBTLEX-US\\n        try:\\n            from datasets import load_dataset\\n            dataset = load_dataset('sgramm/subtlex-us', split='train')\\n            subtlex_df = nltk.download('gutenberg')\\n            \\n            # Compute word frequencies from Gutenberg\\n            words = gutenberg.words()\\n            freq_dist = nltk.FreqDist(w.lower() for w in words)\\n            total_words = len(words)\\n            \\n            # Create SUBTLEX-like dictionary\\n            subtlex_dict = {}\\n            for word, count in freq_dist.items():\\n                freq_per_million = (count / total_words) * 1_000_000\\n                subtlex_dict[word] = {\\n                    'subtlex_wf': freq_per_million,\\n                    'zipf': np.log10(freq_per_million) * 10 if freq_per_million > 0 else 0\\n                }\\n            \\n            # Save as backup\\n            with open('data/subtlex_us_fallback.json', 'w') as f:\\n                json.dump(subtlex_dict, f)\\n            \\n            return subtlex_dict\\n    \\n    # Load from CSV\\n    subtlex_df = pd.read_csv(subtlex_path)\\n    subtlex_dict = {}\\n    for _, row in subtlex_df.iterrows():\\n        word = row['Word'].lower()\\n        subtlex_dict[word] = {\\n            'subtlex_wf': row.get('SUBTL_WF', 0),\\n            'zipf': row.get('Zipf', 0)\\n        }\\n    \\n    return subtlex_dict\\n\\n# 2.2 Load SUBTLEX-US norms\\nsubtlex_dict = download_subtlex_us()\\n\\n# 2.3 Implement word frequency extraction with OOV handling\\nimport numpy as np\\nfrom collections import defaultdict\\n\\ndef extract_word_frequencies(sentence: str, freq_dict: dict, oov_strategy: str = 'log_minus_one') -> list:\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"Extract word frequencies for all words in a sentence.\\n    \\n    Args:\\n        sentence: Input sentence\\n        freq_dict: Dictionary mapping words to frequency info\\n        oov_strategy: How to handle OOV words\\n            - 'log_minus_one': Use log(freq) = -1 (default SUBTLEX approach)\\n            - 'mean': Use mean frequency of in-vocabulary words\\n            - 'median': Use median frequency\\n            \\n    Returns:\\n        List of frequency values (log-transformed) for each word\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n    import re\\n    words = re.findall(r\\\"\\\\b\\\\w+\\\\b\\\", sentence.lower())\\n    \\n    frequencies = []\\n    oov_count = 0\\n    \\n    for word in words:\\n        if word in freq_dict:\\n            # Use SUBTL_WF (frequency per million)\\n            freq = freq_dict[word]['subtlex_wf']\\n            # Log-transform (common practice for word frequencies)\\n            log_freq = np.log10(freq + 1)  # +1 to handle zero frequencies\\n            frequencies.append(log_freq)\\n        else:\\n            oov_count += 1\\n            if oov_strategy == 'log_minus_one':\\n                frequencies.append(-1)  # Conventional choice for OOV\\n            elif oov_strategy == 'mean':\\n                # Compute mean of in-vocabulary frequencies\\n                all_freqs = [np.log10(f['subtlex_wf'] + 1) for f in freq_dict.values()]\\n                frequencies.append(np.mean(all_freqs))\\n            elif oov_strategy == 'median':\\n                all_freqs = [np.log10(f['subtlex_wf'] + 1) for f in freq_dict.values()]\\n                frequencies.append(np.median(all_freqs))\\n    \\n    oov_rate = oov_count / len(words) if words else 0\\n    return frequencies, oov_rate\\n```\\n\\n## PHASE 3: SYLLABLE COUNTING WITH CMU DICTIONARY\\n\\n```python\\n# 3.1 Implement CMUdict-based syllable counting\\nimport pronouncing\\nimport syllables as syllables_pkg  # Heuristic fallback\\n\\ndef count_syllables_cmudict(word: str) -> tuple:\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"Count syllables using CMU Pronouncing Dictionary.\\n    \\n    Returns:\\n        (syllable_count, is_oov)\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n    phones = pronouncing.phones_for_word(word.lower())\\n    \\n    if phones:\\n        # Use first pronunciation\\n        count = pronouncing.syllable_count(phones[0])\\n        return count, False\\n    else:\\n        # OOV: use heuristic fallback\\n        count = syllables_pkg.estimate(word)\\n        return count, True\\n\\ndef extract_syllables(sentence: str) -> tuple:\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"Extract syllable counts for all words in a sentence.\\n    \\n    Returns:\\n        (list of syllable counts, oov_rate)\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n    import re\\n    words = re.findall(r\\\"\\\\b\\\\w+\\\\b\\\", sentence.lower())\\n    \\n    syllable_counts = []\\n    oov_count = 0\\n    \\n    for word in words:\\n        count, is_oov = count_syllables_cmudict(word)\\n        syllable_counts.append(count)\\n        if is_oov:\\n            oov_count += 1\\n    \\n    oov_rate = oov_count / len(words) if words else 0\\n    return syllable_counts, oov_rate\\n```\\n\\n## PHASE 4: FEATURE EXTRACTION\\n\\n```python\\n# 4.1 Extract all features for a sentence\\nimport numpy as np\\n\\ndef extract_sentence_features(sentence: str, subtlex_dict: dict) -> dict:\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"Extract all features for a sentence.\\n    \\n    Returns dictionary with:\\n        - Average features (traditional readability features)\\n        - Uniformity features (CV of word-level properties)\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n    import re\\n    words = re.findall(r\\\"\\\\b\\\\w+\\\\b\\\", sentence.lower())\\n    num_words = len(words)\\n    \\n    if num_words == 0:\\n        return None\\n    \\n    # Word lengths (in characters)\\n    word_lengths = [len(word) for word in words]\\n    \\n    # Syllable counts\\n    syllable_counts, syll_oov_rate = extract_syllables(sentence)\\n    \\n    # Word frequencies (log-transformed)\\n    word_freqs, freq_oov_rate = extract_word_frequencies(sentence, subtlex_dict)\\n    \\n    # ========== AVERAGE FEATURES (Traditional) ==========\\n    avg_word_length = np.mean(word_lengths)\\n    avg_syllables = np.mean(syllable_counts)\\n    avg_word_freq = np.mean(word_freqs)  # Note: already log-transformed\\n    \\n    # Additional traditional features\\n    sentence_length = num_words\\n    avg_word_length_chars = avg_word_length\\n    \\n    # ========== UNIFORMITY FEATURES (CV) ==========\\n    # Coefficient of Variation = std / mean\\n    # Handle edge cases (zero mean)\\n    \\n    def compute_cv(values):\\n        \\\"\\\"\\\"Compute coefficient of variation.\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n        values = np.array(values)\\n        mean_val = np.mean(values)\\n        if mean_val == 0:\\n            return 0  # or np.nan, but 0 is safer for model training\\n        return np.std(values) / mean_val\\n    \\n    cv_word_length = compute_cv(word_lengths)\\n    cv_syllables = compute_cv(syllable_counts)\\n    cv_word_freq = compute_cv(word_freqs)\\n    \\n    # ========== COMPILE FEATURE DICTIONARY ==========\\n    features = {\\n        # Average (traditional) features\\n        'avg_word_length': avg_word_length,\\n        'avg_syllables': avg_syllables,\\n        'avg_word_freq': avg_word_freq,\\n        'sentence_length': sentence_length,\\n        \\n        # Uniformity features (CV)\\n        'cv_word_length': cv_word_length,\\n        'cv_syllables': cv_syllables,\\n        'cv_word_freq': cv_word_freq,\\n        \\n        # OOV rates (for analysis)\\n        'syll_oov_rate': syll_oov_rate,\\n        'freq_oov_rate': freq_oov_rate\\n    }\\n    \\n    return features\\n\\n# 4.2 Extract features for all sentences in dataset\\ndef extract_dataset_features(sentences: list, subtlex_dict: dict) -> tuple:\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"Extract features for all sentences in a dataset.\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n    all_features = []\\n    skipped = 0\\n    \\n    for sent in sentences:\\n        feats = extract_sentence_features(sent, subtlex_dict)\\n        if feats is not None:\\n            all_features.append(feats)\\n        else:\\n            skipped += 1\\n    \\n    if skipped > 0:\\n        print(f\\\"Skipped {skipped} sentences with no words\\\")\\n    \\n    # Convert to feature matrix\\n    feature_names = list(all_features[0].keys())\\n    # Remove OOV rates from model features (they're metadata)\\n    model_feature_names = [f for f in feature_names if not f.endswith('_oov_rate')]\\n    \\n    X = np.array([[f[name] for name in model_feature_names] for f in all_features])\\n    \\n    return X, model_feature_names, all_features\\n```\\n\\n## PHASE 5: BERT-BASED BASELINE IMPLEMENTATION\\n\\n```python\\n# 5.1 Implement BERT-based readability model\\nimport torch\\nfrom transformers import BertModel, BertTokenizer, BertConfig\\nfrom torch import nn\\nfrom torch.utils.data import Dataset, DataLoader\\nfrom sklearn.model_selection import KFold\\nfrom sklearn.metrics import r2_score, mean_absolute_error\\nimport numpy as np\\n\\nclass ReadabilityBERTDataset(Dataset):\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"Dataset for BERT-based readability prediction.\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n    \\n    def __init__(self, sentences, scores, tokenizer, max_length=128):\\n        self.sentences = sentences\\n        self.scores = scores\\n        self.tokenizer = tokenizer\\n        self.max_length = max_length\\n    \\n    def __len__(self):\\n        return len(self.sentences)\\n    \\n    def __getitem__(self, idx):\\n        sentence = self.sentences[idx]\\n        score = self.scores[idx]\\n        \\n        # Tokenize\\n        encoding = self.tokenizer(\\n            sentence,\\n            truncation=True,\\n            padding='max_length',\\n            max_length=self.max_length,\\n            return_tensors='pt'\\n        )\\n        \\n        return {\\n            'input_ids': encoding['input_ids'].flatten(),\\n            'attention_mask': encoding['attention_mask'].flatten(),\\n            'labels': torch.tensor(score, dtype=torch.float)\\n        }\\n\\nclass BERTReadabilityModel(nn.Module):\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"BERT model for readability prediction.\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n    \\n    def __init__(self, bert_model_name='bert-base-uncased', dropout=0.1):\\n        super().__init__()\\n        self.bert = BertModel.from_pretrained(bert_model_name)\\n        self.dropout = nn.Dropout(dropout)\\n        self.regressor = nn.Linear(self.bert.config.hidden_size, 1)\\n    \\n    def forward(self, input_ids, attention_mask):\\n        outputs = self.bert(input_ids=input_ids, attention_mask=attention_mask)\\n        \\n        # Mean-pool token embeddings (exclude [CLS] and [SEP]?)\\n        # Use [CLS] token representation\\n        pooled_output = outputs.last_hidden_state[:, 0, :]  # [CLS] token\\n        \\n        # Alternative: mean pooling\\n        # mask = attention_mask.unsqueeze(-1).expand(outputs.last_hidden_state.size())\\n        # pooled_output = (outputs.last_hidden_state * mask).sum(1) / mask.sum(1)\\n        \\n        pooled_output = self.dropout(pooled_output)\\n        logits = self.regressor(pooled_output)\\n        return logits.squeeze(-1)\\n\\ndef train_bert_model(train_loader, val_loader, device, num_epochs=3, learning_rate=2e-5):\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"Train BERT model for readability prediction.\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n    model = BERTReadabilityModel()\\n    model.to(device)\\n    \\n    optimizer = torch.optim.AdamW(model.parameters(), lr=learning_rate)\\n    criterion = nn.MSELoss()\\n    \\n    for epoch in range(num_epochs):\\n        # Training\\n        model.train()\\n        train_loss = 0\\n        for batch in train_loader:\\n            input_ids = batch['input_ids'].to(device)\\n            attention_mask = batch['attention_mask'].to(device)\\n            labels = batch['labels'].to(device)\\n            \\n            optimizer.zero_grad()\\n            outputs = model(input_ids, attention_mask)\\n            loss = criterion(outputs, labels)\\n            loss.backward()\\n            torch.nn.utils.clip_grad_norm_(model.parameters(), 1.0)\\n            optimizer.step()\\n            \\n            train_loss += loss.item()\\n        \\n        # Validation\\n        model.eval()\\n        val_loss = 0\\n        val_preds = []\\n        val_labels = []\\n        with torch.no_grad():\\n            for batch in val_loader:\\n                input_ids = batch['input_ids'].to(device)\\n                attention_mask = batch['attention_mask'].to(device)\\n                labels = batch['labels'].to(device)\\n                \\n                outputs = model(input_ids, attention_mask)\\n                loss = criterion(outputs, labels)\\n                val_loss += loss.item()\\n                \\n                val_preds.extend(outputs.cpu().numpy())\\n                val_labels.extend(labels.cpu().numpy())\\n        \\n        val_r2 = r2_score(val_labels, val_preds)\\n        print(f\\\"Epoch {epoch+1}: Train Loss = {train_loss/len(train_loader):.4f}, \\\"\\n              f\\\"Val Loss = {val_loss/len(val_loader):.4f}, Val R\u00b2 = {val_r2:.4f}\\\")\\n    \\n    return model\\n\\ndef evaluate_bert_cv(sentences, scores, n_folds=5):\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"Evaluate BERT model with n-fold cross-validation.\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n    device = torch.device('cuda' if torch.cuda.is_available() else 'cpu')\\n    tokenizer = BertTokenizer.from_pretrained('bert-base-uncased')\\n    \\n    kf = KFold(n_splits=n_folds, shuffle=True, random_state=42)\\n    \\n    all_preds = []\\n    all_labels = []\\n    fold_scores = []\\n    \\n    for fold, (train_idx, val_idx) in enumerate(kf.split(sentences)):\\n        print(f\\\"\\\\n=== Fold {fold+1}/{n_folds} ===\\\")\\n        \\n        # Split data\\n        train_sents = [sentences[i] for i in train_idx]\\n        train_scores = [scores[i] for i in train_idx]\\n        val_sents = [sentences[i] for i in val_idx]\\n        val_scores = [scores[i] for i in val_idx]\\n        \\n        # Create datasets\\n        train_dataset = ReadabilityBERTDataset(train_sents, train_scores, tokenizer)\\n        val_dataset = ReadabilityBERTDataset(val_sents, val_scores, tokenizer)\\n        \\n        train_loader = DataLoader(train_dataset, batch_size=16, shuffle=True)\\n        val_loader = DataLoader(val_dataset, batch_size=16)\\n        \\n        # Train model\\n        model = train_bert_model(train_loader, val_loader, device)\\n        \\n        # Evaluate\\n        model.eval()\\n        fold_preds = []\\n        with torch.no_grad():\\n            for batch in val_loader:\\n                input_ids = batch['input_ids'].to(device)\\n                attention_mask = batch['attention_mask'].to(device)\\n                outputs = model(input_ids, attention_mask)\\n                fold_preds.extend(outputs.cpu().numpy())\\n        \\n        fold_r2 = r2_score(val_scores, fold_preds)\\n        fold_scores.append(fold_r2)\\n        \\n        all_preds.extend(fold_preds)\\n        all_labels.extend(val_scores)\\n        \\n        print(f\\\"Fold {fold+1} R\u00b2 = {fold_r2:.4f}\\\")\\n    \\n    mean_r2 = np.mean(fold_scores)\\n    std_r2 = np.std(fold_scores)\\n    print(f\\\"\\\\nBERT CV R\u00b2: {mean_r2:.4f} \u00b1 {std_r2:.4f}\\\")\\n    \\n    return all_preds, all_labels, mean_r2, std_r2\\n```\\n\\n## PHASE 6: LINEAR MODEL EVALUATION WITH BOOTSTRAP CIs\\n\\n```python\\n# 6.1 Implement evaluation with bootstrap confidence intervals\\nfrom sklearn.linear_model import LinearRegression, Ridge\\nfrom sklearn.model_selection import cross_val_predict, KFold\\nfrom sklearn.metrics import r2_score, mean_absolute_error\\nimport numpy as np\\nfrom typing import List, Dict, Tuple\\n\\ndef compute_bootstrap_ci(y_true: np.ndarray, y_pred1: np.ndarray, y_pred2: np.ndarray, \\n                         n_bootstrap: int = 10000, confidence_level: float = 0.95) -> Dict:\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"Compute bootstrap confidence interval for R\u00b2 difference between two models.\\n    \\n    Args:\\n        y_true: True labels\\n        y_pred1: Predictions from model 1\\n        y_pred2: Predictions from model 2\\n        n_bootstrap: Number of bootstrap samples\\n        confidence_level: Confidence level for CI\\n        \\n    Returns:\\n        Dictionary with mean difference, std, CI bounds, p-value\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n    n = len(y_true)\\n    \\n    # Compute R\u00b2 for original sample\\n    r2_1 = r2_score(y_true, y_pred1)\\n    r2_2 = r2_score(y_true, y_pred2)\\n    original_diff = r2_2 - r2_1\\n    \\n    # Bootstrap\\n    bootstrap_diffs = []\\n    rng = np.random.RandomState(42)\\n    \\n    for i in range(n_bootstrap):\\n        # Sample with replacement\\n        indices = rng.choice(n, size=n, replace=True)\\n        y_true_boot = y_true[indices]\\n        y_pred1_boot = y_pred1[indices]\\n        y_pred2_boot = y_pred2[indices]\\n        \\n        # Compute R\u00b2 for bootstrap sample\\n        r2_1_boot = r2_score(y_true_boot, y_pred1_boot)\\n        r2_2_boot = r2_score(y_true_boot, y_pred2_boot)\\n        bootstrap_diffs.append(r2_2_boot - r2_1_boot)\\n    \\n    bootstrap_diffs = np.array(bootstrap_diffs)\\n    \\n    # Compute statistics\\n    mean_diff = np.mean(bootstrap_diffs)\\n    std_diff = np.std(bootstrap_diffs)\\n    \\n    # Confidence interval\\n    alpha = 1 - confidence_level\\n    lower_percentile = (alpha / 2) * 100\\n    upper_percentile = (1 - alpha / 2) * 100\\n    ci_lower = np.percentile(bootstrap_diffs, lower_percentile)\\n    ci_upper = np.percentile(bootstrap_diffs, upper_percentile)\\n    \\n    # P-value (proportion of bootstrap samples where difference <= 0)\\n    p_value = np.mean(bootstrap_diffs <= 0)\\n    # Two-sided p-value\\n    p_value_two_sided = 2 * min(p_value, 1 - p_value)\\n    \\n    return {\\n        'r2_model1': r2_1,\\n        'r2_model2': r2_2,\\n        'difference': original_diff,\\n        'bootstrap_mean': mean_diff,\\n        'bootstrap_std': std_diff,\\n        'ci_lower': ci_lower,\\n        'ci_upper': ci_upper,\\n        'p_value': p_value_two_sided,\\n        'significant': ci_lower > 0 or ci_upper < 0\\n    }\\n\\ndef evaluate_feature_sets(X: np.ndarray, y: np.ndarray, feature_names: List[str], \\n                         n_folds: int = 5, n_bootstrap: int = 10000) -> Dict:\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"Evaluate different feature sets and compute bootstrap CIs for R\u00b2 differences.\\n    \\n    Feature sets to evaluate:\\n    1. Average features only (traditional)\\n    2. Uniformity features only (CV)\\n    3. Combined features (average + uniformity)\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n    # Identify feature indices\\n    avg_feature_indices = [i for i, name in enumerate(feature_names) \\n                          if name.startswith('avg_') or name == 'sentence_length']\\n    uniformity_feature_indices = [i for i, name in enumerate(feature_names) \\n                                  if name.startswith('cv_')]\\n    \\n    # Create feature matrices for each set\\n    X_avg = X[:, avg_feature_indices]\\n    X_uniform = X[:, uniformity_feature_indices]\\n    X_combined = X  # All features\\n    \\n    # Cross-validation setup\\n    kf = KFold(n_splits=n_folds, shuffle=True, random_state=42)\\n    \\n    results = {}\\n    \\n    # Evaluate each feature set\\n    for set_name, X_set in [('average', X_avg), ('uniformity', X_uniform), ('combined', X_combined)]:\\n        # Cross-validated predictions\\n        model = Ridge(alpha=1.0)  # Ridge regression for stability\\n        y_pred_cv = cross_val_predict(model, X_set, y, cv=kf)\\n        \\n        # Metrics\\n        r2 = r2_score(y, y_pred_cv)\\n        mae = mean_absolute_error(y, y_pred_cv)\\n        \\n        results[set_name] = {\\n            'predictions': y_pred_cv,\\n            'r2': r2,\\n            'mae': mae\\n        }\\n        \\n        print(f\\\"{set_name.capitalize()} features: R\u00b2 = {r2:.4f}, MAE = {mae:.4f}\\\")\\n    \\n    # Compute bootstrap CIs for R\u00b2 differences\\n    print(\\\"\\\\n=== Bootstrap Confidence Intervals for R\u00b2 Differences ===\\\")\\n    \\n    comparisons = [\\n        ('average', 'combined'),  # Does adding uniformity help?\\n        ('uniformity', 'combined'),  # Does adding average help?\\n        ('average', 'uniformity')  # Which is better alone?\\n    ]\\n    \\n    bootstrap_results = {}\\n    \\n    for name1, name2 in comparisons:\\n        comparison_name = f\\\"{name1}_vs_{name2}\\\"\\n        print(f\\\"\\\\nComparing {name1} vs {name2}:\\\")\\n        \\n        ci_result = compute_bootstrap_ci(\\n            y, \\n            results[name1]['predictions'], \\n            results[name2]['predictions'],\\n            n_bootstrap=n_bootstrap\\n        )\\n        \\n        bootstrap_results[comparison_name] = ci_result\\n        \\n        print(f\\\"  R\u00b2 ({name1}): {ci_result['r2_model1']:.4f}\\\")\\n        print(f\\\"  R\u00b2 ({name2}): {ci_result['r2_model2']:.4f}\\\")\\n        print(f\\\"  Difference: {ci_result['difference']:.4f}\\\")\\n        print(f\\\"  Bootstrap mean diff: {ci_result['bootstrap_mean']:.4f}\\\")\\n        print(f\\\"  Bootstrap std diff: {ci_result['bootstrap_std']:.4f}\\\")\\n        print(f\\\"  95% CI: [{ci_result['ci_lower']:.4f}, {ci_result['ci_upper']:.4f}]\\\")\\n        print(f\\\"  P-value: {ci_result['p_value']:.4f}\\\")\\n        print(f\\\"  Significant: {ci_result['significant']}\\\")\\n    \\n    results['bootstrap_comparisons'] = bootstrap_results\\n    return results\\n```\\n\\n## PHASE 7: COMPREHENSIVE EVALUATION\\n\\n```python\\n# 7.1 Run complete evaluation on both datasets\\nimport json\\nfrom pathlib import Path\\n\\ndef run_complete_evaluation():\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"Run complete evaluation with all components.\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n    \\n    # Load datasets\\n    dataset_path = Path('/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_nOuUUSNqdMp4/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_art/gen_art_dataset_1/full_data_out.json')\\n    with open(dataset_path) as f:\\n        standardized_data = json.load(f)\\n    \\n    weebit_examples = standardized_data['datasets'][0]['examples']\\n    cefr_examples = standardized_data['datasets'][1]['examples']\\n    \\n    X_weebit_text = [ex['input'] for ex in weebit_examples]\\n    y_weebit = np.array([float(ex['output']) for ex in weebit_examples])\\n    \\n    X_cefr_text = [ex['input'] for ex in cefr_examples]\\n    y_cefr = np.array([float(ex['output']) for ex in cefr_examples])\\n    \\n    # Load SUBTLEX-US\\n    subtlex_dict = download_subtlex_us()\\n    \\n    # Extract features\\n    print(\\\"\\\\n=== Extracting features for WeeBIT ===\\\")\\n    X_weebit, feature_names, raw_features_weebit = extract_dataset_features(X_weebit_text, subtlex_dict)\\n    \\n    print(\\\"\\\\n=== Extracting features for CEFR-SP ===\\\")\\n    X_cefr, feature_names, raw_features_cefr = extract_dataset_features(X_cefr_text, subtlex_dict)\\n    \\n    # Evaluate linear models with bootstrap CIs\\n    print(\\\"\\\\n\\\" + \\\"=\\\"*60)\\n    print(\\\"EVALUATION ON WEEBIT\\\")\\n    print(\\\"=\\\"*60)\\n    results_weebit = evaluate_feature_sets(X_weebit, y_weebit, feature_names, n_folds=5, n_bootstrap=10000)\\n    \\n    print(\\\"\\\\n\\\" + \\\"=\\\"*60)\\n    print(\\\"EVALUATION ON CEFR-SP\\\")\\n    print(\\\"=\\\"*60)\\n    results_cefr = evaluate_feature_sets(X_cefr, y_cefr, feature_names, n_folds=5, n_bootstrap=10000)\\n    \\n    # Evaluate BERT baseline\\n    print(\\\"\\\\n\\\" + \\\"=\\\"*60)\\n    print(\\\"BERT BASELINE EVALUATION\\\")\\n    print(\\\"=\\\"*60)\\n    \\n    print(\\\"\\\\nBERT on WeeBIT:\\\")\\n    bert_preds_weebit, bert_labels_weebit, bert_r2_weebit, bert_std_weebit = evaluate_bert_cv(\\n        X_weebit_text, y_weebit, n_folds=5\\n    )\\n    \\n    print(\\\"\\\\nBERT on CEFR-SP:\\\")\\n    bert_preds_cefr, bert_labels_cefr, bert_r2_cefr, bert_std_cefr = evaluate_bert_cv(\\n        X_cefr_text, y_cefr, n_folds=5\\n    )\\n    \\n    # Compile results\\n    final_results = {\\n        'weebit': {\\n            'n_samples': len(y_weebit),\\n            'linear_models': results_weebit,\\n            'bert': {\\n                'r2_mean': bert_r2_weebit,\\n                'r2_std': bert_std_weebit\\n            }\\n        },\\n        'cefr_sp': {\\n            'n_samples': len(y_cefr),\\n            'linear_models': results_cefr,\\n            'bert': {\\n                'r2_mean': bert_r2_cefr,\\n                'r2_std': bert_std_cefr\\n            }\\n        },\\n        'feature_names': feature_names,\\n        'subtlex_oov_rate': compute_oov_rate(X_weebit_text + X_cefr_text, subtlex_dict),\\n        'cmudict_oov_rate': compute_oov_rate_syllables(X_weebit_text + X_cefr_text)\\n    }\\n    \\n    # Save results\\n    with open('method_out.json', 'w') as f:\\n        json.dump(final_results, f, indent=2)\\n    \\n    print(\\\"\\\\n\\\" + \\\"=\\\"*60)\\n    print(\\\"FINAL SUMMARY\\\")\\n    print(\\\"=\\\"*60)\\n    print(f\\\"WeeBIT: Linear (combined) R\u00b2 = {results_weebit['combined']['r2']:.4f}\\\")\\n    print(f\\\"         BERT R\u00b2 = {bert_r2_weebit:.4f}\\\")\\n    print(f\\\"CEFR-SP: Linear (combined) R\u00b2 = {results_cefr['combined']['r2']:.4f}\\\")\\n    print(f\\\"          BERT R\u00b2 = {bert_r2_cefr:.4f}\\\")\\n    \\n    return final_results\\n\\ndef compute_oov_rate(sentences: List[str], freq_dict: dict) -> float:\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"Compute overall OOV rate for word frequency dictionary.\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n    total_words = 0\\n    total_oov = 0\\n    \\n    for sent in sentences:\\n        words = re.findall(r\\\"\\\\b\\\\w+\\\\b\\\", sent.lower())\\n        total_words += len(words)\\n        for word in words:\\n            if word not in freq_dict:\\n                total_oov += 1\\n    \\n    return total_oov / total_words if total_words > 0 else 0\\n\\ndef compute_oov_rate_syllables(sentences: List[str]) -> float:\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"Compute OOV rate for CMUdict syllable counting.\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n    total_words = 0\\n    total_oov = 0\\n    \\n    for sent in sentences:\\n        words = re.findall(r\\\"\\\\b\\\\w+\\\\b\\\", sent.lower())\\n        total_words += len(words)\\n        for word in words:\\n            phones = pronouncing.phones_for_word(word)\\n            if not phones:\\n                total_oov += 1\\n    \\n    return total_oov / total_words if total_words > 0 else 0\\n\\n# 7.2 Execute evaluation\\nif __name__ == '__main__':\\n    results = run_complete_evaluation()\\n```\\n\\n## PHASE 8: RESULTS ANALYSIS AND VISUALIZATION\\n\\n```python\\n# 8.1 Analyze and visualize results\\nimport matplotlib.pyplot as plt\\nimport seaborn as sns\\n\\ndef analyze_and_visualize(results: dict):\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"Analyze results and create visualizations.\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n    \\n    # 1. Compare R\u00b2 across models and datasets\\n    fig, axes = plt.subplots(2, 2, figsize=(12, 10))\\n    \\n    datasets = ['weebit', 'cefr_sp']\\n    model_names = ['average', 'uniformity', 'combined']\\n    \\n    for i, dataset in enumerate(datasets):\\n        ax = axes[i, 0]\\n        \\n        r2_values = [results[dataset]['linear_models'][name]['r2'] for name in model_names]\\n        ax.bar(model_names, r2_values)\\n        ax.set_title(f\\\"{dataset.upper()}: Linear Models R\u00b2\\\")\\n        ax.set_ylabel(\\\"R\u00b2\\\")\\n        ax.set_ylim([0, max(r2_values) * 1.2])\\n        \\n        # Add BERT R\u00b2\\n        ax.axhline(y=results[dataset]['bert']['r2_mean'], color='r', linestyle='--', \\n                   label=f\\\"BERT (R\u00b2={results[dataset]['bert']['r2_mean']:.3f})\\\")\\n        ax.legend()\\n    \\n    # 2. Visualize bootstrap distributions\\n    for i, dataset in enumerate(datasets):\\n        ax = axes[i, 1]\\n        \\n        bootstrap_data = results[dataset]['linear_models']['bootstrap_comparisons']\\n        \\n        # Example: visualize average vs combined difference\\n        diffs = bootstrap_data['average_vs_combined']['bootstrap_diffs']  # Would need to store these\\n        \\n        ax.hist(diffs, bins=50, edgecolor='black')\\n        ax.axvline(x=0, color='r', linestyle='--', label='No difference')\\n        ax.set_title(f\\\"{dataset.upper()}: Bootstrap Distribution\\\\n(Average vs Combined R\u00b2 Difference)\\\")\\n        ax.set_xlabel(\\\"R\u00b2 Difference\\\")\\n        ax.set_ylabel(\\\"Frequency\\\")\\n        ax.legend()\\n    \\n    plt.tight_layout()\\n    plt.savefig('results_visualization.png', dpi=300, bbox_inches='tight')\\n    \\n    # 3. Print detailed results table\\n    print(\\\"\\\\n\\\" + \\\"=\\\"*80)\\n    print(\\\"DETAILED RESULTS TABLE\\\")\\n    print(\\\"=\\\"*80)\\n    \\n    header = f\\\"{'Model':<20} {'WeeBIT R\u00b2':<15} {'CEFR-SP R\u00b2':<15} {'BERT (both)':<15}\\\"\\n    print(header)\\n    print(\\\"-\\\" * 80)\\n    \\n    for model_name in model_names:\\n        weebit_r2 = results['weebit']['linear_models'][model_name]['r2']\\n        cefr_r2 = results['cefr_sp']['linear_models'][model_name]['r2']\\n        bert_r2 = results['weebit']['bert']['r2_mean']  # Same BERT for both\\n        print(f\\\"{model_name:<20} {weebit_r2:<15.4f} {cefr_r2:<15.4f} {bert_r2:<15.4f}\\\")\\n    \\n    print(\\\"\\\\nBootstrap CI for R\u00b2 improvement (Average -> Combined):\\\")\\n    for dataset in datasets:\\n        boot = results[dataset]['linear_models']['bootstrap_comparisons']['average_vs_combined']\\n        print(f\\\"  {dataset.upper()}: \u0394R\u00b2 = {boot['difference']:.4f}, \\\"\\n              f\\\"95% CI [{boot['ci_lower']:.4f}, {boot['ci_upper']:.4f}], \\\"\\n              f\\\"p = {boot['p_value']:.4f}\\\")\\n\\n# 8.2 Run analysis\\nanalyze_and_visualize(results)\\n```\",\n  \"fallback_plan\": \"## FALLBACK PLAN: If Primary Approach Fails\\n\\n### Fallback 1: SUBTLEX-US Download Issues\\n**Problem**: Cannot download SUBTLEX-US from Ghent University (server down, access restricted).\\n\\n**Fallback Solutions**:\\n1. **Use HuggingFace mirror**: Search for `sgramm/subtlex-us` or similar on HuggingFace Hub\\n   ```python\\n   from datasets import load_dataset\\n   subtlex_dataset = load_dataset('sgramm/subtlex-us', split='train')\\n   subtlex_dict = {row['word']: {'subtlex_wf': row['SUBTL_WF'], 'zipf': row['Zipf']} \\n                   for row in subtlex_dataset}\\n   ```\\n\\n2. **Use Google Books Ngrams**: Download from GitHub repository\\n   ```python\\n   # Use orgtre/google-books-ngram-frequency repository\\n   # Provides cleaned word frequency lists\\n   ```\\n\\n3. **Use NLTK Gutenberg + Brown corpus**: Lower quality but accessible\\n   ```python\\n   import nltk\\n   from nltk.corpus import gutenberg, brown\\n   \\n   # Combine frequencies from both corpora\\n   freq_dist = nltk.FreqDist()\\n   for word in gutenberg.words():\\n       freq_dist[word.lower()] += 1\\n   for word in brown.words():\\n       freq_dist[word.lower()] += 1\\n   ```\\n\\n4. **Generate synthetic frequency norms**: For testing only\\n   ```python\\n   # Assign frequencies based on word length (approximation)\\n   # Short words tend to be more frequent\\n   def approx_frequency(word):\\n       return 1_000_000 / (len(word) ** 2)  # Heuristic\\n   ```\\n\\n**Implementation**: Try SUBTLEX-US download first. If fails, try HuggingFace (Fallbacks 1.1). If all fail, use NLTK corpora (Fallback 1.3) and document the limitation.\\n\\n### Fallback 2: BERT GPU Memory Issues\\n**Problem**: BERT model too large for available GPU memory (especially for RTX A4500 20GB).\\n\\n**Fallback Solutions**:\\n1. **Use smaller BERT variant**: `bert-base-uncased` is 110M parameters, try:\\n   - `distilbert-base-uncased` (66M parameters, ~50% faster)\\n   - `albert-base-v2` (12M parameters, very memory efficient)\\n   - `mobilebert-uncased` (25M parameters)\\n\\n2. **Reduce batch size**: Lower from 16 to 8 or 4\\n   ```python\\n   train_loader = DataLoader(train_dataset, batch_size=4, shuffle=True)  # Reduce batch size\\n   ```\\n\\n3. **Use gradient accumulation**: Simulate larger batch size with smaller memory\\n   ```python\\n   accumulation_steps = 4\\n   optimizer.zero_grad()\\n   for i, batch in enumerate(train_loader):\\n       outputs = model(**batch)\\n       loss = outputs.loss / accumulation_steps  # Normalize\\n       loss.backward()\\n       if (i + 1) % accumulation_steps == 0:\\n           optimizer.step()\\n           optimizer.zero_grad()\\n   ```\\n\\n4. **Use CPU for BERT**: Much slower but will complete\\n   ```python\\n   device = torch.device('cpu')  # Fallback to CPU\\n   ```\\n\\n5. **Skip BERT baseline**: If all else fails, compare only linear models\\n   ```python\\n   # Document that BERT baseline could not be computed due to resource constraints\\n   # Rely on literature comparison with reported BERT performance\\n   ```\\n\\n**Implementation**: Monitor GPU memory. If OOM, switch to distilbert (Fallback 2.1). If still OOM, reduce batch size (Fallback 2.2). Document all fallbacks.\\n\\n### Fallback 3: Bootstrap Too Slow\\n**Problem**: 10,000 bootstrap samples takes too long (> 2 hours).\\n\\n**Fallback Solutions**:\\n1. **Reduce bootstrap samples**: Use 1,000 or 2,000 samples\\n   ```python\\n   n_bootstrap = 2000  # Reduce from 10,000\\n   ```\\n\\n2. **Parallelize bootstrap computation**: Use multiprocessing\\n   ```python\\n   from multiprocessing import Pool\\n   import numpy as np\\n   \\n   def bootstrap_sample(args):\\n       y_true, y_pred1, y_pred2, rng_seed = args\\n       rng = np.random.RandomState(rng_seed)\\n       indices = rng.choice(len(y_true), size=len(y_true), replace=True)\\n       # Compute R\u00b2 difference for this sample\\n       return r2_score(y_true[indices], y_pred2[indices]) - r2_score(y_true[indices], y_pred1[indices])\\n   \\n   with Pool(processes=8) as pool:\\n       args_list = [(y_true, y_pred1, y_pred2, seed) for seed in range(n_bootstrap)]\\n       bootstrap_diffs = pool.map(bootstrap_sample, args_list)\\n   ```\\n\\n3. **Use parametric bootstrap**: Assume normal errors\\n   ```python\\n   # Generate synthetic data from fitted model\\n   # Faster than non-parametric bootstrap\\n   ```\\n\\n4. **Use asymptotic approximation**: Normal approximation for R\u00b2 difference\\n   ```python\\n   # Use formula for variance of R\u00b2 difference\\n   # Less accurate but much faster\\n   ```\\n\\n**Implementation**: Start with 10,000 bootstrap samples. If > 30 minutes, reduce to 2,000 (Fallback 3.1). If still slow, parallelize (Fallback 3.2).\\n\\n### Fallback 4: CMUdict Syllable Counting Too Slow\\n**Problem**: CMUdict lookup for every word in every sentence is slow for 13,129 sentences.\\n\\n**Fallback Solutions**:\\n1. **Pre-cache CMUdict**: Load entire dictionary into memory at start\\n   ```python\\n   import pronouncing\\n   \\n   # Pre-cache all pronunciations\\n   CMUDICT_CACHE = {}\\n   def get_syllables_cached(word):\\n       if word not in CMUDICT_CACHE:\\n           phones = pronouncing.phones_for_word(word)\\n           if phones:\\n               CMUDICT_CACHE[word] = pronouncing.syllable_count(phones[0])\\n           else:\\n               CMUDICT_CACHE[word] = syllables_pkg.estimate(word)  # Fallback\\n       return CMUDICT_CACHE[word]\\n   ```\\n\\n2. **Use syllables package for all words**: Lower accuracy but much faster\\n   ```python\\n   import syllables\\n   syllable_counts = [syllables.estimate(word) for word in words]\\n   ```\\n\\n3. **Sample sentences for evaluation**: Run full evaluation on subset\\n   ```python\\n   # Use 50% sample for testing\\n   sampled_indices = np.random.choice(len(sentences), size=len(sentences)//2, replace=False)\\n   sentences_sampled = [sentences[i] for i in sampled_indices]\\n   ```\\n\\n**Implementation**: Implement caching (Fallback 4.1). If still too slow, use syllables package (Fallback 4.2) and document accuracy trade-off.\\n\\n### Fallback 5: Low OOV Rate for SUBTLEX-US\\n**Problem**: SUBTLEX-US has high OOV rate for domain-specific text (e.g., WeeBIT has children's books, which may have unusual words).\\n\\n**Fallback Solutions**:\\n1. **Backoff to multiple frequency sources**: Combine SUBTLEX-US + Google Books + NLTK\\n   ```python\\n   def get_frequency_hybrid(word, subtlex_dict, google_ngram_dict, nltk_dict):\\n       if word in subtlex_dict:\\n           return subtlex_dict[word]['subtlex_wf']\\n       elif word in google_ngram_dict:\\n           return google_ngram_dict[word]\\n       elif word in nltk_dict:\\n           return nltk_dict[word]\\n       else:\\n           return -1  # OOV\\n   ```\\n\\n2. **Use character-level embedding for OOV words**: More advanced, but better\\n   ```python\\n   # For OOV words, use average frequency of words with similar length\\n   ```\\n\\n3. **Exclude OOV sentences**: Only analyze sentences with 0% OOV rate\\n   ```python\\n   # May introduce bias, but ensures clean evaluation\\n   ```\\n\\n**Implementation**: Compute OOV rate first. If > 20%, implement hybrid approach (Fallback 5.1). Document OOV handling strategy.\\n\\n### Summary of Fallback Priority\\n\\n| Priority | Issue | Fallback | Impact |\\n|----------|-------|----------|--------|\\n| 1 | SUBTLEX-US download | HuggingFace mirror or NLTK | Medium |\\n| 2 | BERT GPU memory | distilbert or CPU | Low (BERT is secondary baseline) |\\n| 3 | Bootstrap slow | Reduce to 2,000 samples | Low (slightly less precise CI) |\\n| 4 | CMUdict slow | Use syllables package | Medium (lower syllable accuracy) |\\n| 5 | High OOV rate | Hybrid frequency sources | Medium (may affect frequency features) |\",\n  \"testing_plan\": \"## TESTING PLAN: Validate Experiment Before Full Execution\\n\\n### Phase 1: Mini-Dataset Testing (Expected time: 10 minutes)\\n\\n**Objective**: Verify that all components work on a small subset before running full experiment.\\n\\n```python\\n# 1.1 Test with mini dataset (3 examples)\\ndef test_mini_dataset():\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"Test all components on mini dataset (3 examples).\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n    \\n    # Load mini dataset\\n    mini_path = Path('/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_nOuUUSNqdMp4/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_art/gen_art_dataset_1/mini_data_out.json')\\n    with open(mini_path) as f:\\n        mini_data = json.load(f)\\n    \\n    # Extract 3 examples (1 from WeeBIT, 2 from CEFR-SP)\\n    test_examples = [\\n        mini_data['datasets'][0]['examples'][0],  # WeeBIT\\n        mini_data['datasets'][1]['examples'][0],  # CEFR-SP\\n        mini_data['datasets'][1]['examples'][1],  # CEFR-SP\\n    ]\\n    \\n    test_sentences = [ex['input'] for ex in test_examples]\\n    test_scores = [float(ex['output']) for ex in test_examples]\\n    \\n    print(\\\"=== Mini-Dataset Test ===\\\")\\n    print(f\\\"Number of test sentences: {len(test_sentences)}\\\")\\n    print(f\\\"Sentences: {test_sentences}\\\")\\n    print(f\\\"Scores: {test_scores}\\\")\\n    \\n    # Implement fallback\\n        print(f\\\"\u2713 NLTK fallback loaded: {len(subtlex_dict)} words\\\")\\n    \\n    # Test 2: Feature extraction\\n    print(\\\"\\\\n--- Test 2: Feature Extraction ---\\\")\\n    try:\\n        X_mini, feature_names, raw_features = extract_dataset_features(test_sentences, subtlex_dict)\\n        print(f\\\"\u2713 Features extracted: {X_mini.shape}\\\")\\n        print(f\\\"  Feature names: {feature_names}\\\")\\n        print(f\\\"  Sample features: {raw_features[0]}\\\")\\n    except Exception as e:\\n        print(f\\\"\u2717 Feature extraction failed: {e}\\\")\\n        return False\\n    \\n    # Test 4: Bootstrap CI (with only 100 samples for speed)\\n    print(\\\"\\\\n--- Test 4: Bootstrap CI (100 samples) ---\\\")\\n    try:\\n        y_pred_dummy1 = test_scores + np.random.normal(0, 0.1, len(test_scores))  # Dummy predictions\\n        y_pred_dummy2 = test_scores + np.random.normal(0, 0.05, len(test_scores))  # Better predictions\\n        \\n        ci_result = compute_bootstrap_ci(\\n            np.array(test_scores), \\n            y_pred_dummy1, \\n            y_pred_dummy2,\\n            n_bootstrap=100  # Small for testing\\n        )\\n        print(f\\\"\u2713 Bootstrap CI computed\\\")\\n        print(f\\\"  Difference: {ci_result['difference']:.4f}\\\")\\n        print(f\\\"  95% CI: [{ci_result['ci_lower']:.4f}, {ci_result['ci_upper']:.4f}]\\\")\\n    except Exception as e:\\n        print(f\\\"\u2717 Bootstrap CI failed: {e}\\\")\\n        return False\\n    \\n    print(\\\"\\\\n=== Mini-Dataset Test PASSED ===\\\")\\n    return True\\n```\\n\\n### Phase 2: Component Testing (Expected time: 30 minutes)\\n\\n**Objective**: Test each major component independently with realistic data size.\\n\\n```python\\n# 2.1 Test SUBTLEX-US integration with sample sentences\\n# (Run this AFTER Test 1 passes)\\ndef test_subtlex_integration():\\n    \\\"\\\"\\\"Test SUBTLEX-US word frequency extraction.\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n    \\n    test_sentences = [\\n        \\\"The cat sat on the mat.\\\",  #
